Leadership and Arrangement in Janazah Prayer
Ruling on who leads and how to line up multiple bodies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the order of precedence for leading Janazah prayer and describe the correct arrangement of multiple bodies in combined prayer and burial according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The most virtuous leads; multiple bodies are arranged in a single row with chests toward the imam in the order: men, boys, hermaphrodites, women.

This passage from Nur al-Idah provides clear guidance on two practical questions: who should lead the Janazah prayer, and how to handle multiple funerals. The Hanafi school prioritizes virtue (al-afdal) in leadership, and for multiple bodies, a specific spatial arrangement preserves dignity and order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for leadership precedence
The text begins with 'أحق الناس بالصلاة على الجنازة' – the most entitled person to lead is the most virtuous. Understand that this means the imam should be chosen based on piety, knowledge, and precedence in good deeds.
This is a general principle in Hanafi fiqh: al-afdal fa al-afdal.
2
Learn the preference for individual prayer over combined
The text states 'فالإفراد بالصلاة لكل منها أولى' – it is better to pray for each Janazah individually. Recognize that combined prayer is permissible but less optimal.
Individual prayer avoids potential confusion in arrangement and respects each deceased.
3
Understand the arrangement of bodies in combined prayer
When combined, the bodies are placed in a long row parallel to the qibla, with the chest of each deceased facing the imam. The order is: men closest to the imam, then boys, then hermaphrodites, then women.
'صفا طويلا مما يلى القبلة' indicates the row is perpendicular to the qibla.
4
Note the reversal for burial
For burial in a single grave, the order is reversed: women first (closest to qibla), then hermaphrodites, then boys, then men. This ensures the most honored (men) are farthest from the qibla side of the grave.
This matches the practice at the Battle of Uhud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحق الناس بالصلاة على الجنازة ويقدم الأفضل فالأفضل، وإن اجتمعت الجنائز فالإفراد بالصلاة لكل منها أولى، وإن اجتمعن وصلى عليها مرة جعلها صفا طويلا مما يلى القبلة بحيث يكون صدر كل قُدّام الإمام، وراعى الترتيب، فيجعل الرجال مما يلى الإمام، ثم الصبيان، بعدهم ثم الخناثى، ثم النساء، ولو دفنوا بقبر واحد وضعوا على عكس هذا.
The most entitled people to pray over the funeral: the best is given precedence. If multiple funerals gather, it is better to pray for each individually. If they gather and one prays over them once, he makes them a long row toward the qibla such that the chest of each is in front of the imam, and he observes the order: he places the men next to the imam, then the boys, then the hermaphrodites, then the women. If they are buried in one grave, they are placed in the opposite of this.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage from Nur al-Idah is a ruling statement establishing the order of precedence for leading Janazah prayer and the arrangement of multiple bodies. According to the Hanafi school, the most virtuous person (in terms of piety, knowledge, etc.) leads. If there are multiple Janazahs, it is preferable to pray for each separately. When combined, they are placed in a long row perpendicular to the qibla, with the chest of each deceased facing the imam. The order respects seniority: men first, then boys, then hermaphrodites, then women. For burial in a single grave, the order is reversed so that the most honored are nearest the qibla. This matches the practice at the Battle of Uhud.

Key Terms
أحق (ahaqq): Most entitled or worthy to perform an action, here leading prayer.
الأفضل فالأفضل (al-afdal fa al-afdal): Giving precedence to the better, then the next best.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when multiple Janazahs are combined, how are the bodies arranged?
A) In a single row with heads toward qibla, men first then women.
B) In a long row with chests toward imam, men then boys then hermaphrodites then women.
C) In separate rows, one for each gender.
D) Stacked one above the other.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that the most virtuous leads, and multiple bodies are arranged in a long row with chests facing the imam in the order: men, boys, hermaphrodites, women; reversed for burial.

The Latecomer in Janazah Prayer
Ruling for joining Janazah prayer late

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct procedure for a latecomer in Janazah prayer and apply the ruling on missing the prayer after the fourth takbir.

Core Idea
A latecomer in Janazah prayer must wait for the imam's takbir, not join between takbirs.

Joining a congregational Janazah prayer after it has started requires specific steps. Unlike regular prayers, you cannot simply catch up between the imam's movements. This passage from Nur al-Idah explains exactly when to enter, how to follow, and what to do if you arrive too late.

Guided Path
1
Identify when the latecomer should not follow
The text says that if a person finds the imam 'between two takbirs', he should not follow (i.e., not start praying). He must wait for the imam's next takbir.
Between takbirs means he arrives after one takbir but before the next.
2
Determine the correct moment to enter
He waits for the imam's next takbir, enters with that takbir, and then concurs with the imam in the supplication (du'a) that follows.
He says his own tahrimiyyah takbir simultaneously with the imam's next takbir.
3
Know the make-up (qada) procedure
After the imam says the final takbir and salam, the latecomer makes up the missed takbirs and their corresponding du'as before the bier is lifted.
He must complete the sequence of four takbirs with their du'as in order.
4
Understand the exception for those present at the opening
If someone attends exactly when the imam says the opening takbir (tahrimiyyah), he does not wait – he follows immediately.
This is the normal case: the latecomer who catches the start follows normally.
5
Recognize when the prayer is missed
If a person arrives after the imam has recited the fourth takbir (but before salam), he has missed the Janazah prayer according to the correct view (al-sahih). There is an opposing view that he can still complete on his own, but the relied-upon position is that it is missed.
This does not mean he cannot pray Janazah on his own later; it means the congregational prayer is no longer valid for him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقتدي بالإمام من "وجده" بين تكبيرتين، بل ينتظر تكبير الإمام، فيدخل معه، ويوافقه في دعائه، ثم يقضي ما فاته قبل رفع الجنازة، ولا ينتظر تكبير الإمام من حضر تحريمته، ومن حضر بعد التكبيرة الرابعة قبل السلام فاتته الصلاة في الصحيح.
The one who finds the imam between two takbirs does not follow him; rather, he waits for the imam's takbir, enters with him, and concurs with him in his supplication, then makes up what he missed before the bier is lifted. The one who attends the opening takbir does not wait for the imam's takbir. The one who attends after the fourth takbir before the salam has missed the prayer according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage details the rules for a latecomer (masbuq) in Janazah prayer. If a person joins after some takbirs have been said, he must not follow the imam in between two takbirs; instead, he waits for the imam to pronounce the next takbir, then enters and follows in the du'a. After the imam says the final takbir and salam, he then makes up the missed takbirs (and corresponding du'as) before the bier is carried away. If he arrives exactly when the imam is about to start (i.e., he hears the opening takbir), he can start immediately without waiting. If he arrives after the fourth takbir but before the salam, his prayer is considered missed (i.e., invalid) according to the soundest opinion; however, there is another narration from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan that he should say one takbir and then complete three after the imam's salam, and this is the fatwa position.

Key Terms
تحرمية (tahrimiyyah): The opening takbir of the prayer, which makes the prayer begin.
قضى (qada): To make up a missed part of the prayer after the imam finishes.
مسبوق (masbuq): A latecomer who misses part of the congregational prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person joins the Janazah prayer between the first and second takbir, what should he do?
A) Raise his hands and follow immediately.
B) Wait for the imam's next takbir, then enter.
C) Leave and wait for the next Janazah.
D) Recite takbir on his own and then leave.

Key Takeaway
A latecomer in Janazah prayer must wait for the imam's next takbir to join, and if he arrives after the fourth takbir, he has missed the prayer.

Dislike of Janazah Prayer in the Mosque
Ruling on Janazah prayer in mosque and its causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing Janazah prayer inside a mosque and distinguish between the two categories of dislike (tanzih and tahrim) based on the underlying cause.

Core Idea
Janazah prayer is disliked in the mosque, but the degree depends on the cause: fear of soiling (tahrim) or occupying the mosque (tanzih).

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on performing Janazah prayer inside a congregational mosque. The school holds it is generally disliked, but the nature of that dislike is nuanced, depending on the specific reason behind the prohibition. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the ruling in various practical scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What specific act is being ruled upon? It is the performance of Janazah prayer inside a mosque where the congregation gathers, whether the bier is inside or outside while some people are inside the mosque.
The ruling is that it is disliked (makruh) according to the preferred view.
2
Understand the two levels of dislike
In Hanafi fiqh, 'makruh' can be of two types: tanzih (lightly disliked, closer to permissible) and tahrim (prohibitively disliked, close to haram). Both are mentioned in different narrations.
The terms tanzihiyyah and tahrimiyyah are defined in the key terms.
3
Analyze the causes
The source identifies two possible causes for the dislike: fear of physical impurity (najasa) reaching the mosque, and occupying the mosque for an act it was not built for.
If the cause is fear of soiling, the dislike is tahrim; if it is occupying the mosque, it is tanzih.
4
Note the preferred narration
The muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam preferred the narration that the dislikedness is tanzih, which became the relied-upon view.
Later Hanafi scholars further conditioned this on community custom.
5
Consider the practical application
If the community habitually prays Janazah in the mosque and the mosque was built with that in mind, the dislike may be removed entirely.
This shows the dynamic nature of fiqh rulings based on custom (urf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتكره الصلاة عليه في مسجد الجماعة وهو فيه، أو خارجه وبعض الناس في المسجد على المختار، وكراهته تنزيهية في رواية، ورجحها المحقق ابن الهمام، وتحريمية في أخرى، والعلة فيه إن كانت خشية التلويث فهي تحريمية، وإن كانت شغل المسجد بما لم يين له فتنزيهية.
It is disliked to pray over him in a congregational mosque while he is inside it, or outside with some people inside the mosque, according to the preferred view. And its dislikedness is tanzih in one narration, which the Muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam preferred, and tahrim in another. The cause: if it is fear of soiling, then it is tahrim; if it is occupying the mosque for something it was not built for, then it is tanzih.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of praying Janazah inside a mosque. According to the Hanafi school, it is disliked (makruh) to perform the Janazah prayer inside a mosque where the congregation gathers, whether the bier is inside or outside while some people are inside. However, there is a difference of opinion on the nature of this dislikedness. One narration considers it tanzih (slightly disliked, closer to permissible), and this was preferred by Ibn al-Humam. Another narration considers it tahrim (prohibited), due to the risk of soiling the mosque. The commentator explains that the degree depends on the cause: if the concern is physical impurity (najasa) reaching the mosque, it becomes prohibited; if it is merely occupying the mosque for an act not intended for it (since mosques are built for prayer and worship, not specifically for funerals), it is tanzih. Later Hanafi scholars like those of al-Wafi conditioned this on custom: if the community habitually prays Janazah in the mosque, it is not disliked, assuming the mosque was built with that knowledge.

Key Terms
تنزيهية (tanzihiyyah): Lightly disliked; avoiding it is better but not sinful.
تحريمية (tahrimiyyah): Praiseworthy disliked; close to haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what determines whether the dislikedness of Janazah prayer in the mosque is tanzih or tahrim?
A) The location of the bier (inside or outside).
B) The cause: fear of soiling or occupying the mosque.
C) The time of day.
D) The number of people present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on Janazah in mosque is not absolute; the dislikedness ranges from tanzih to tahrim depending on whether the cause is occupying the mosque or fear of soiling.

Rulings on Janazah of Infants and Captive Children
Distinction of Janazah rulings based on signs of life and captivity status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for an infant who shows signs of life at birth versus one who does not, and apply the same distinction to the case of a captive child.

Core Idea
The infant who cried at birth is a full Muslim; the stillborn is not prayed over, and the captive child's status follows the parents' Islam.

This passage from Nur al-Idah lays out a critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh: when a newborn dies, the ruling of Janazah depends on whether it showed any sign of life (istihlal). The same reasoning is extended to the unique case of a captive child in Dar al-Harb. Understanding this distinction clarifies how the school defines 'personhood' for funeral rites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two infant categories
Look for the terms 'istahalla' (cried/sign of life) and 'lam yastahil' (did not cry). The ruling hinges on this distinction.
Istihlal includes any sign of life, not just crying.
2
State the ruling for each category
For the infant who cried: naming, washing, Janazah prayer. For the stillborn: washing (preferred view), wrapping, burial without prayer.
The stillborn is washed but not prayed over.
3
Note the preferred view (al-mukhtar)
The text says the stillborn is washed in the preferred view; this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
4
Apply analogy to captive child
A child captured with one non-Muslim parent: not prayed over unless the parent or child becomes Muslim. This mirrors the stillborn's lack of independent religious status.
The child's religion follows the parent of higher status or the dar if captured alone.
5
Identify the exception for child captured alone
If the child is captured without parents, he is considered Muslim by virtue of being in Dar al-Islam, and Janazah prayer is performed.
This is at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن استهل سمي وغسل وصلي عليه وإن لم يستهل غسل في المختار وأدرج في خرقة ودفن و لم يصل عليه، كصبي سبي مع أحد أبويه إلا أن يسلم أحدهما، أو هو الصبي أو لم يسب أحدهما معه.
Whoever cries at birth is named, washed, and prayed over. If he does not cry, he is washed according to the preferred view, wrapped in a cloth, buried, and not prayed over, like a boy captured with one of his parents unless one of them becomes Muslim, or the boy himself [becomes Muslim], or neither of his parents is captured with him.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage covers the Hanafi rulings for an infant who dies shortly after birth. The key distinction is whether the infant showed signs of life (istihlal: crying, moving, etc.) at birth. If the infant cried (or showed any definite life sign), it is treated as a full Muslim: given a name, washed, shrouded, and the Janazah prayer is performed. If the infant did not show any life sign (i.e., stillborn or died during birth), the majority Hanafi view is that it is still washed and wrapped in a cloth and buried, but no Janazah prayer is offered, because it is considered a part of the mother and not a separate person. This also applies if the infant cried but died before fully emerging from the womb. The text then analogizes this to the case of a minor child taken captive along with one of his kafir parents from Dar al-Harb. Such a child is not prayed over unless the parent converts or the child himself reaches an age of understanding and converts. If the child is captured alone (without parents), he is considered Muslim by virtue of the Islamic land and is prayed over.

Key Terms
استهل (istahalla): To cry at birth, indicating life.
سبي (sabi): A child taken captive in war.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a stillborn infant (no sign of life)?
A) Named, washed, and Janazah prayer performed.
B) Washed, wrapped, and buried without Janazah prayer.
C) Buried without washing or prayer.
D) Left to be dealt with by non-Muslims.

Key Takeaway
The presence of a sign of life (istihlal) at birth determines whether the Janazah prayer is performed; similarly, the Islam of a captive child or his parents determines his funeral rite.

Washing a Deceased Kafir Relative
Ruling on washing a kafir relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing a deceased kafir relative and explain the manner of washing and burial.

Core Idea
A kafir relative's body is washed like a filthy cloth, not a ritual ghusl, and then buried simply.

This lesson addresses a common situation: a Muslim has a non-Muslim relative who dies. The Hanafi school provides clear guidance on how to handle the body. The ruling emphasises that no ritual purification (ghusl) is performed, but the body must be washed and buried in a dignified manner to prevent harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relationship and ruling
Determine if the deceased is a kafir and if the washer is a Muslim relative. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah), not obligatory, to wash the body.
The condition for obligation of ghusl is the deceased being Muslim.
2
Understand the method
The washing is like cleaning a soiled cloth: the body is washed thoroughly but without the intention of ritual purification. After washing, it is shrouded in any cloth and buried in a pit or handed over to the deceased's own community.
No specific ritual of janazah is performed.
3
Note what is prohibited
Janazah prayer and ritual ghusl are not to be performed for a kafir, even if they are related. This is because the deceased is not Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان لكافر قريب مسلم غسله كغسل خرقة نجسة، وكفنه في خرقة، وألقاه في حفرة أو دفعه إلى أهل ملته ولا يصلى على باغ وقاطع طريق وإن كان مسلماً قتل في حالة المحاربة، وقاتل بالخنق غيلة، ومكابر في المصر ليلا بالسلاح، ومقتول عصبية وإن غسلوا.
If a kafir has a Muslim relative, he washes him like washing a filthy cloth, shrouds him in a cloth, throws him into a pit or hands him to the people of his faith. And no prayer is performed over a rebel or highway robber, even if he is Muslim, killed in the state of combat, a murderer by strangulation by treachery, one who fights in the city at night with weapons, and one killed in factional fighting, even if they are washed.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage provides two distinct rulings. First, regarding a deceased kafir who has a Muslim relative (such as a child or sibling): the Muslim relative may wash the body, but it is treated like washing a dirty cloth; it is not a ritual ghusl. The body is simply shrouded and buried in a pit or handed over to the kafir's coreligionists. No Janazah prayer is offered. This is not obligatory, as the condition for obligation is the deceased being Muslim. Second, the passage lists categories of Muslims who are not to be prayed over: (1) a rebel (baghi) killed while fighting, (2) a highway robber killed in the act, (3) a murderer who kills by strangulation (ghilah), (4) one who fights with a weapon in the city at night (mukabir), and (5) one killed in factional or 'asabiyyah (tribal) fighting. Even if they are washed, no prayer is performed for them, as a deterrent and because they are treated like non-Muslims in this respect. However, if they were killed after being captured or not in the act, the ruling may differ.

Key Terms
غيلة (ghilah): Murder by treachery, e.g., luring the victim to a secluded place then killing.
باغ (baghi): A rebel who fights against the legitimate Muslim ruler with a ta'wil (interpretation).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how should a Muslim wash his deceased kafir relative?
A) With the full ritual ghusl (ghusl janabat) like a Muslim.
B) Like washing a filthy cloth, without ritual intention.
C) Spraying water without touching the body.
D) Pouring water from a container from a distance.

Key Takeaway
A deceased kafir relative may be washed by a Muslim, but only as a simple cleaning, not a ritual ghusl, and no janazah prayer is offered.

Prohibition of Janazah Prayer on Rebels and Highway Robbers
Ruling on prohibition of Janazah prayer for certain criminals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the categories of Muslims for whom Janazah prayer is prohibited according to the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning behind the prohibition.

Core Idea
Janazah prayer is prohibited for rebels, highway robbers, treacherous murderers, and those killed in factional fighting — even if they are Muslims.

This lesson discusses an exception to the general rule that every Muslim who dies should have Janazah prayer. The Hanafi school identifies certain categories of Muslims who die in state of major sin or combat who are not prayed over. This serves as a deterrent and a form of social censure.

Guided Path
1
List the prohibited categories
Identify each category mentioned: (1) rebel (baghi) killed in battle, (2) highway robber (qati' tariq) killed in the act, (3) murderer by treachery (ghilah), (4) one who fights with weapons in the city at night (mukabir), (5) one killed in factional fighting (asabiyyah).
All are Muslim, but they die while committing serious crimes.
2
Understand the condition
The prohibition applies when they are killed in the very act of their crime. If they are captured and executed later, the ruling may differ.
The text says 'killed in the state of combat' etc.
3
Determine the ruling
The prayer is prohibited (haram) for them, even if their bodies are washed. They are treated like non-Muslims in this regard.
The phrase 'and no prayer is performed' is a clear prohibition.
4
Note the wisdom
The prohibition serves as a deterrent and shows Islamic disapproval of such acts.
It does not preclude making du'a for them privately.

Source Text

Source pages p. 191

beginner
6 min
Ruling on the Newborn Who Shows Signs of Life (Istihlal)
Ruling on newborn with signs of life vs without

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a newborn after birth, identify the legal definition of istihlal, and apply the condition of most of the body emerging.

Core Idea
A newborn who shows signs of life gets full funeral rites; otherwise only washing and burial without prayer.

This passage from Nur al-Idah and its commentary clarifies the Hanafi position on funeral rites for a newborn who dies soon after birth. The key distinction is whether the newborn showed signs of life (istihlal) after most of the body emerged. Understanding this ruling involves both the legal definition of istihlal and the condition of emergence, which determines whether the funeral prayer is wajib or omitted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) for a newborn who dies. The ruling differs based on whether the newborn cried (mustahal) or not. For mustahal, prayer is wajib; for non-mustahal, no prayer.
The main text states: 'ومن استهل سمي وغسل وصلي عليه' – whoever cries is named, washed, and prayed upon.
2
Define istihlal legally
The commentary clarifies that istihlal is not limited to crying; it includes any sign of life such as movement or sound. The literal meaning of 'raising the voice' is not a condition.
The text says: 'المراد معناه الشرعي أي وجد منه حال ولادته حياة بحركة أو صوت'.
3
Apply the condition of emergence
The ruling applies only after most of the newborn's body has emerged. If most of the body (chest if head-first, navel if feet-first) has not emerged, even if the baby cries, it is treated as not having demonstrated life independent of the mother.
The commentary states: 'إن نزل برأسه مستقيما، أو سرته إن خرج برجليه منكوسا' – criterion for most of the body.
4
Understand the status of the non-mustahal
The newborn that does not show signs of life is considered like a part of the mother's body. It is washed, wrapped, and buried, but no prayer is performed. It does not inherit nor is inherited from, by consensus.
The hashiah says: 'المختار: وظاهر الرواية منع الكل، وكذا لا يرث ولا يورث اتفاقا؛ لأنه كجزء الحي'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحق الناس بالصلاة على الجنازة ومن استهل سمي وغسل وصلي عليه وإن لم يستهل غسل في المختار وأدرج في خرقة ودفن و لم يصل عليه. ومن استهل إلخ: هو بالبناء للفاعل، وأصل الاستهلال في اللغة رفع الصوت واستهل الهلال بالبناء للمفعول إذا أبصر . ولا يخفى أن المناسب هنا المعنى الأول، إلا أن خصوص رفع الصوت ليس بشرط بل المراد معناه الشرعي أي وجد منه حال ولادته حياة بحركة أو صوت، وقد خرج أكثره وصدره إن نزل برأسه مستقيما، أو سرته إن خرج برجليه منكوسا. [ مراقي الفلاح: ٥٩٧] وإن لم يستهل إلخ مثله ما إذا استهل فمات قبل خروج أكثره، وأما الاستهلال في البطن فغير معتبر بالأولى. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٥٩٨] المختار: وظاهر الرواية منع الكل، وكذا لا يرث ولا يورث اتفاقا؛ لأنه كجزء الحي. [حاشية الطحطاوي: ٥٩٨]
Who is most entitled to lead the funeral prayer? And whoever cries at birth is named, washed, and prayed upon. And if he does not cry, according to the preferred view, he is washed, wrapped in a cloth, and buried without prayer. Commentary: The word 'mustahal' is from the active particle, originally in language meaning raising the voice, but here the intended meaning is the legal one: that life appeared from him by movement or sound. If most of his body, his chest if he emerged head-first, or his navel if he emerged feet-first, has come out. [Maraqi al-Falah: 597] 'And if he does not cry' etc. applies similarly if he cried but died before most of him came out. Crying in the womb is not considered. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi: 598] The preferred view and the evident transmission is to prevent all, and he does not inherit nor is inherited from, by consensus, because he is like a part of the living. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi: 598]
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for a newborn who cries at birth (mustahal) and one who does not. The main text declares that a crying newborn is given a name, washed, and prayed over; a non-crying newborn only washed and buried without prayer. The commentary clarifies that 'mustahal' in legal context means any sign of life—movement or sound—not necessarily a cry. It also specifies that the ruling applies only after most of the body has emerged; crying before that is irrelevant. The comment also notes that according to the preferred view (al-mukhtar) and the zahir al-riwayah, the non-crying newborn is considered like a limb of the mother—neither inheriting nor being inherited from.

Key Terms
الاستهلال (al-istihlal): Legal term for the newborn showing signs of life after birth, such as crying, movement, or sound.
المختار (al-mukhtar): The preferred view (relied upon for fatwa) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following newborns is NOT prayed upon?
A) A newborn who cries after most of the body has emerged.
B) A newborn who moves an arm after the navel has emerged (breech birth).
C) A newborn who cries before most of the body emerges and then dies.
D) A newborn who makes a sound after full head-first emergence.

Key Takeaway
A newborn must both show a sign of life (istihlal) and have most of its body emerge for the funeral prayer to be wajib.

Ruling on Funeral Rites for a Captive Child with a Polytheist Parent
Ruling on janazah for a captive child with one polytheist parent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing funeral prayer for a captive child of polytheist parents, list the three exceptional conditions that change the ruling, and cite the Quranic proof (5:51) against Muslim guardianship over a disbeliever.

Core Idea
A captive child with a polytheist parent remains a non-Muslim until conversion—prayer is prohibited because no Muslim can be guardian over a disbeliever.

This passage from Nur al-Idah addresses a specific scenario: a child captured from dar al-harb along with one polytheist parent. The default ruling is that such a child is treated according to the parent's religion, so no funeral prayer is performed over him if he dies. However, there are three exceptions that make the child entitled to Muslim rites. The commentary strengthens the ruling by refuting a problematic opinion and citing Quran 5:51.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The funeral prayer over a captive child of polytheist parents is not performed; the child follows the parent's faith.
The child is considered to have the religion of the parent with whom he was captured.
2
List the three exceptions
(a) one parent converts to Islam, (b) the child himself converts if he is sane and understands Islam, (c) the child is captured alone without either parent.
In case (c), the child becomes Muslim by virtue of being in dar al-Islam.
3
Understand the underlying principle
A child's religion is tied to his parents' religion or the land of residence. Captivity with a parent preserves the parent's religion; capture alone severs that tie.
This principle distinguishes between children captured with or without parents.
4
Examine the Quranic proof
Surah al-Ma'idah 5:51 prohibits taking Jews and Christians as awliya (allies/guardians). This negates any claim of Muslim guardianship over a disbeliever, including for funeral rites.
The verse is used to reject the idea that a Muslim relative can be a guardian for a disbeliever's funeral.
5
Refute the flawed counter-view
Some scholars argue that a Muslim relative (e.g., sister, uncle) can be guardian over a disbeliever's funeral. The author calls this 'defective' because true guardianship (wilayah) requires religious affinity, which is negated by the verse.
The term 'relative' is broad, but does not override the prohibition of taking disbelievers as awliya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كصبي سبي مع أحد أبويه إلا أن يسلم أحدهما، أو هو أو بالغ أو مجنون الصبي أو لم يسب أحدهما معه. كصبي سبي إلخ أي كما لا يصلى على صبي أسر مع أحد أبويه الكافرين من دار الحرب، ثم مات، فلا يصلى عليه، إلا أن يصير أحد الأبوين مسلماً، أو نفسه وهو مقيد بشرط أن يعقل صفة الإسلام، أو سبي هو ولم يسب أبوه أو أمه . فيكون مسلماً تبعاً للدار قريب هذا أحسن مما قاله بعضهم من أنه إذا مات الكافر وله ولي مسلم" فإنها عبارة معيبة؛ لأن حقيقة الولاية منتفية، قال الله تعالى: ولا تَتَّخِذُوا الْيَهُودَ وَالنَّصَارَى أَوْلِيَاءَ [المائدة: ٥١]، وأطلق القريب فشمل ذوي الأرحام كالأخت والخال والخالة.
Like a child captured with one of his two parents, unless one of them becomes Muslim, or the child himself (if he is mature or insane) or if one parent was not captured with him. Commentary: It means just as no prayer is performed for a child captured with one of his polytheist parents from dar al-harb, then dies; so no prayer is performed, unless one parent becomes Muslim, or he himself (conditioned that he understands Islam), or if the child alone is captured and neither parent was taken. Then he becomes Muslim by virtue of the land (dar). This is better than what some said: 'If a disbeliever dies and has a Muslim relative' — that expression is defective because true guardianship is negated. Allah said: 'Do not take the Jews and Christians as allies.' [Al-Ma'idah: 51]. He used the word 'relative' broadly, including uterine relatives like sister, maternal uncle, and aunt.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage deals with the funeral status of a child captured from dar al-harb along with one polytheist parent. The main ruling states that such a child is treated like a disbeliever unless one of the parents accepts Islam, or the child himself (if mentally capable) accepts, or if the child is captured alone (without either parent). In the last case, the child is considered Muslim by virtue of being in dar al-Islam. The commentary refutes the idea that a Muslim relative can be a guardian for a disbeliever, citing Quran 5:51 that prohibits taking disbelievers as allies. The term 'relative' here includes all relatives, even those with only uterine relation.

Key Terms
سبي (sabiyy): A child taken captive from dar al-harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is a captive child from dar al-harb given Muslim funeral rites according to this passage?
A) Always, because he is in dar al-Islam.
B) Only if both parents accept Islam.
C) If one parent or the child accepts Islam, or if the child is captured alone.
D) Never, because he follows his parents' religion.

Key Takeaway
A captive child with a polytheist parent is treated as a non-Muslim unless one of three conditions is met; the Quran prohibits Muslim guardianship over disbelievers.

Washing a Non-Muslim Relative: Permissibility and Procedure
Ruling on a Muslim washing a disbelieving relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a Muslim washing a disbelieving relative and identify the conditions that make it permissible rather than obligatory.

Core Idea
Washing a disbelieving relative is permissible physical cleaning, not an act of worship.

This lesson examines a unique ruling where a Muslim may perform ghusl for a non-Muslim relative. Unlike the ritual washing of a Muslim deceased, this is purely a physical cleansing, like washing a soiled cloth. The passage clarifies that it is not wajib because the obligation of ghusl requires the deceased to be Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a Muslim washing the body of a disbelieving relative. The ruling is permissible (mubah), not obligatory.
2
Understand the procedural comparison
The text compares it to washing a dirty cloth, emphasizing physical cleaning without ritual significance.
No takbir or specific du'a is mentioned.
3
Recognize the condition for obligation
The commentary states that one condition for ghusl to be wajib is the deceased being Muslim. Since the relative is a disbeliever, it is not wajib.
The obligation of ghusl (fard kifayah) only applies to Muslims.
4
Note the scope of 'relative'
The term 'qarib' includes dhawi al-arham (uterine relatives like maternal aunt/uncle), even though they are not inheritors by faraid.
This expands the ruling beyond direct blood relations.
5
Consider preferable practice
If the disbeliever has another disbelieving relative, it is better for the Muslim to avoid performing the washing themselves.
The permissibility remains, but avoidance is recommended to maintain distance from non-Muslim practices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان لكافر قريب مسلم غسله كغسل خرقة نجسة، وكفنه في خرقة، وألقاه في حفرة أو دفعه إلى أهل ملته. غسله: أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان له قريب غيره كافراً أو لا، غير أنه إن كان فالأولى للمسلم تجنبه، وشمل القريب ذوي الأرحام، وليس الغسل واجباً عليه؛ لأن من شرط الوجوب إسلام الميت. [حاشية الطحطاوي بتغيير وتصرف: ٦٠٠]
And if a disbeliever has a Muslim relative, he washes him like washing a dirty cloth, shrouds him in a cloth, throws him into a pit, or hands him over to the people of his religion. Commentary: 'He washes him' is general, includes whether he has another disbelieving relative or not. However, if there is one, it is better for the Muslim to avoid it. The word 'relative' includes those connected through women (dhawu al-arham). And this washing is not obligatory on him because one condition for obligation is the deceased being Muslim. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi with modification: 600]
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage permits a Muslim to wash the body of a disbelieving relative, comparing it to washing a dirty cloth—purely a physical cleaning with no ritual significance. The Muslim may also shroud and bury the body, or hand it over to the disbeliever's coreligionists. The commentary clarifies that this is not obligatory (wajib) because the obligation of ghusl requires the deceased to be Muslim. The term 'relative' (qarib) includes even uterine relatives (dhawu al-arham) like a maternal aunt. If the disbeliever has other disbelieving relatives, it is better for the Muslim to avoid performing the washing himself.

Key Terms
ذوي الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives connected through females, such as maternal aunt, uncle, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not obligatory (wajib) for a Muslim to wash a disbelieving relative's body?
A) Because the relative is a disbeliever.
B) Because washing the dead is only sunnah.
C) Because one condition for obligation is the deceased being Muslim.
D) Because it is better to avoid contact with impurity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a Muslim to wash a non-Muslim relative as physical cleaning, but it is not obligatory due to the condition of Islam for ritual ghusl.

No Funeral Prayer for Rebels and Highway Robbers
Ruling with expansion on exceptions to funeral prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the categories of Muslims for whom funeral prayer is not performed according to the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning for each.

Core Idea
Funeral prayer is not performed for Muslims who die while engaged in active rebellion, highway robbery, treachery, or partisanship.

While funeral prayer (salat al-janazah) is a collective duty (fard kifayah) for deceased Muslims, the Hanafi school identifies specific exceptions. This passage from Nur al-Idah enumerates those for whom the prayer is not performed, even though they are outwardly Muslims. Understanding these exceptions clarifies the boundaries of communal obligation and the gravity of certain sins.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
The text begins with a clear prohibition: 'ولا يصلى' – no prayer is performed over rebels and highway robbers. Note that this applies even if they are Muslims.
The ruling is not an absolute denial of their faith but a consequence of their state at death.
2
List the five categories
Extract from the passage: (1) baghi (rebel) killed in war, (2) qati' al-tariq (highway robber) killed in the act, (3) qatil al-ghilah (one who kills by treachery/strangulation), (4) mukabir (one who operates aggressively in the city at night with weapons), and (5) maqtul 'asabiyyah (one killed due to partisanship).
The text expands from the initial two categories to include three more through commentary.
3
Understand the commentary expansions
The commentary defines 'ghilah' as assassination by deception, and 'mukabir' as one who uses force. It also notes that people of partisanship may not even be washed, according to some Hanafi views.
These definitions clarify the specific circumstances that deprive one of the prayer.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a modern case: armed rebels attack a military base and are killed. According to this passage, the Hanafi school would not perform the funeral prayer for them, even if they were Muslims.
This is not a judgment of their ultimate fate, but a legal ruling based on their apparent state at death.
5
Reflect on the underlying wisdom
The denial of prayer is a legal consequence of dying in a state of active rebellion, treachery, or partisanship – actions that indicate a departure from faith at the moment of death.
The ruling underscores the importance of dying in a state of submission to Allah and the legitimate Muslim authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصلى على باغ وقاطع طريق وإن كان مسلماً قتل في حالة المحاربة، وقاتل بالخنق غيلة، ومكابر في المصر ليلا بالسلاح، ومقتول عصبية وإن غسلوا، أي للتعصب والحمية. غيلة بالكسر الاغتيال، يقال: قتله أن يخدعه فيذهب به إلى موضع فيقتله والمراد أعم كما لو خنقه في منزل. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٠٢] ومكابر: إذا قتل في تلك الحالة. مراقي (الفلاح وإن غسلوا : اعلم أن عبارة مسكين تفيد أن أهل العصبية لا يغسلون. [حاشية الطحطاوي بزيادة: ٦٠٣]
And no prayer is performed over a rebel or a highway robber, even if Muslim, who is killed in a state of war, and the one who kills by strangulation treacherously, and one who operates aggressively in the city at night with weapons, and one killed in partisanship, even if they are washed—meaning due to partisanship and zeal. 'Ghilah': with kasra, meaning assassination, said: he kills him by deceiving him, taking him to a place, then killing him; the intended meaning is more general, like strangling him in a house. [Maraqi al-Falah: 602] 'And one who operates aggressively': if he is killed in that state. [Maraqi al-Falah] 'Even if they are washed': know that the expression 'miskin' indicates that the people of partisanship are not washed. [Hashiyah al-Tahtawi with addition: 603]
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage lists categories of Muslims who are not given funeral prayer. They include: rebels (baghi) and highway robbers (qati' al-tariq) killed in battle; those who kill by strangulation treacherously (ghilah); those who operate aggressively in the city at night with weapons (mukabir); and those killed in partisanship (asabiyyah). Even if their bodies are washed, they are not prayed upon. The commentary defines 'ghilah' as assassination by deception, and 'mukabir' as one who uses force. A note adds that people of partisanship are not even washed, according to some statements. The reason for denying prayer is that these are considered corrupt and their deaths are not in a state of faith.

Key Terms
باغ (baghi): Rebel: one who rises against the legitimate Muslim ruler with a ta'wil (interpretation).
غيلة (ghilah): Assassination by treachery, e.g., luring a victim and killing them.
مكابر (mukabir): One who uses force or weapons aggressively, especially at night in a city.
عصبية (asabiyyah): Partisanship, zealotry, often tribal or factional loyalty leading to violence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT among those who are not prayed upon according to this passage?
A) A rebel killed in battle.
B) A highway robber killed in the act.
C) A Muslim who dies in a natural disaster.
D) One killed while committing murder by strangulation treacherously.

Key Takeaway
Funeral prayer is not performed for Muslims killed in active rebellion, highway robbery, treachery, night aggression, or partisanship, according to the Hanafi school.

Funeral Prayer for Suicide and Parricide: Hanafi Ruling
Intra-Hanafi dispute on funeral prayer for suicide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on performing funeral prayer for a suicide victim and distinguish between the opinions of the Sahibayn and Abu Yusuf, citing the key dalil.

Core Idea
A deliberate suicide is washed and prayed over; the Prophet's refusal is not a general rule.

This passage addresses a sensitive but important fiqh issue: whether a person who intentionally commits suicide receives the full funeral rites. The text presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). Understanding this debate reveals how the Hanafi school weighs conflicting dala'il – the principle that a Muslim's blood is inviolable versus the analogy to rebels. Additionally, the passage covers the ruling for one who kills a parent unjustly.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling in the matn
The matn states that a person who commits suicide is washed (yughassalu) and prayed over (yusalla 'alayhi). The commentary clarifies this applies to intentional suicide, not accidental or due to severe pain.
The ruling in the matn is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
There is a report of a difference between Abu Yusuf and his two companions (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf, he is not prayed over; according to the Sahibayn, he is prayed over.
This ikhtilaf is specific to the prayer; washing is agreed upon.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's dalil
Abu Yusuf analogizes the suicide to a rebel (baghi) because he is an oppressor (zalim) against himself. He also cites the hadith from Sahih Muslim where the Prophet ﷺ did not pray over a suicide.
The hadith: a man killed himself with arrows; the Prophet did not pray over him.
4
Examine the Sahibayn's dalil
The Sahibayn argue that the suicide's blood is hādir (wasted), meaning no blood-money or retaliation is due. Therefore, he is like one who dies naturally (ḥatf anfih) and deserves the full rites.
The principle: a Muslim's blood is inviolable except where the law explicitly denies rights.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is the view of the Sahibayn: the funeral prayer is performed for the suicide. The hadith is acknowledged but interpreted as the Prophet’s personal choice or abrogated, or it was for tanzīh (discouragement).
For parricide: one who kills a parent unjustly is not prayed over, but this does not apply if the parent was a harbī or rebel.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقاتل نفسه يغسل ويصلى عليه لا على قاتل أحد أبويه عمدا. وقاتل نفسه: أراد به قاتل نفسه عمداً لا لشدة وجع، فخرج بمفهومه الخطأ فإنه يغسل ويصلى عليه. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٠٢] ويصلى عليه أي من قتل نفسه عمداً اختلف فيه المشايخ قيل: يصلى عليه، وقيل: لا، ومنهم من حكى فيه خلافاً بين أبي يوسف وصاحبيه، فعنده: لا يصلى عليه وعندهما يصلى عليه، لأبي يوسف: إنه ظالم بالقتل، فيلحق بالباغي ولهما أن دمه هدر، فصار كما لو مات حتف أنفه، وفي صحيح مسلم ما يؤيد قول أبي يوسف: عن جابر بن سمرة قال: أتي النبي برجل قتل نفسه بمشاقص، فلم يصل عليه. [فتح القدير: ١٠٩/٢] قاتل أحد أبويه أراد به من قتل أباه أو أمه ظلماً؛ لأن من قتل أباه الحربي أو أمه الحربية أو أباه الباغي، فليس عليه شيء من الإثم
And the one who kills himself is washed and prayed over; but not the one who kills one of his parents intentionally. Commentary: 'The one who kills himself' means intentionally, not due to severe pain; excluded is accidental killing, for he is washed and prayed over. [Maraqi al-Falah: 602] 'And prayed over' — concerning one who kills himself intentionally, the scholars differed: it is said he is prayed over, and it is said not. Some related a difference between Abu Yusuf and his two companions (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): according to him (Abu Yusuf), he is not prayed over; according to them, he is prayed over. For Abu Yusuf: he is an oppressor by killing, so he joins the rebel. For them: his blood is shed without compensation, so it is as if he died naturally. In Sahih Muslim, there is support for Abu Yusuf's view: from Jabir ibn Samurah: a man who killed himself with arrows was brought to the Prophet ﷺ, and he did not pray over him. [Fath al-Qadir: 2/109] 'One who kills one of his parents' means one who kills his father or mother unjustly; because one who kills his father who is a harbi or his mother who is a harbiyya, or his father who is a rebel, has no sin.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage states the ruling for suicide and parricide. A deliberate suicide (not due to unbearable pain) is washed and prayed over, but there is ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds he is not prayed upon, comparing him to a rebel, while the Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold he is, since his blood is wasted like natural death. The commentary cites a hadith supporting Abu Yusuf. For parricide (killing one's parent unjustly), funeral prayer is not performed. However, if the parent is a harbi (enemy combatant) or rebel, the killer incurs no sin, implying the restriction applies only to unjust killing of Muslim parents.

Key Terms
قاتل نفسه (qatil nafsihi): A person who commits intentional suicide, not due to unbearable pain or accidentally.
قاتل أحد أبويه عمدا (qatil ahad abawayhi amdan): A person who intentionally kills one of his parents unjustly.
دمه هدر (damuhu hadar): His blood is wasted, meaning no blood-money or retaliation is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, which of the following is true about a person who commits suicide?
A) He is not washed or prayed upon.
B) He is washed but not prayed upon.
C) He is washed and prayed upon.
D) He is not washed but prayed upon.

Key Takeaway
A deliberate suicide is washed and prayed over in the Hanafi school; the Prophet's refusal to pray is not a binding precedent.

Sunnah Practices for Carrying the Bier and Digging the Grave
Sunnah rulings on carrying the bier and grave depth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah methods for carrying the bier, identify the recommended pace, and specify the preferred depth of the grave.

Core Idea
Carrying the bier is a communal obligation done in a specific sunnah manner: on shoulders, briskly, behind the bier.

This lesson covers the sunnah rulings related to the funeral procession and burial. While carrying and burying the deceased is a communal obligation (fard kifayah), the manner in which it is done follows specific sunnah guidelines. Understanding these details ensures the act is performed with the proper respect and reward.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah acts
List the matters that are sunnah or mustahabb in the funeral procession: number of carriers, order, pace, and walking position.
The passage mentions four carriers, forty steps, specific order, brisk pace, and walking behind.
2
Understand the carrier order
Memorize the sequence: front right on carrier's right (meaning the bier's right is toward the carrier's left when standing facing the bier), then back right, then front left on left shoulder, then back left on left shoulder.
'Its right side is toward the carrier's left' means the carrier's right shoulder touches the bier's right side.
3
Note prohibited and disliked actions
Identify actions that are makruh: raising voice in dhikr, jogging (khabab), sitting before placing the bier down.
Khabab is a pace that causes the body to shake; it is prohibited or disliked.
4
Apply the grave depth ruling
The recommended depth is half a man's height or up to the chest; deeper is better. This applies to normal soil.
The depth ensures the body is covered and not disturbed.

[مراقي الفلاح: ٦٠٣] وينبغي حملها إلخ اعلم أن أصل الحمل والدفن فرض كفاية، ولذا لا يجوز أخذ الأجرة على ذلك إذا تعينوا. Section on carrying it and burying it. It is sunnah for four men to carry it, and it is recommended to carry it forty steps, starting with its front right side on the carrier's right side (its right is the side towards the carrier's left), then its back right side on his right, then its front left side on his left (on his left shoulder), then ending with the left side on his left shoulder. It is mustahabb to walk quickly without jogging, which would cause the body to shake. Walking behind is better than walking in front, like the superiority of obligatory prayer over supererogatory. It is makruh to raise the voice in remembrance or to sit before placing it down. The grave is dug half a man's height or to the chest, and if increased it is good. And Quran... Commentary
This passage outlines the sunnah method for carrying the bier and digging the grave. It is recommended for four men to carry the bier, each bearing a corner, with a specific order: front right, back right, front left, back left. They should walk briskly (not running) to avoid jostling the body. Walking behind the bier is more meritorious. Raising the voice in dhikr or sitting before the body is placed down is disliked. The grave should be dug to a depth of half a man's height or up to the chest; deeper is better. The commentary clarifies that carrying and burial is a communal obligation (fard kifayah), so no fee may be taken if the people are sufficient. It is an act of worship, and the Prophet himself carried the bier of Sa'd ibn Mu'adh.

Key Terms
خبب (khabab): A fast pace between walking and running, which would cause the body to shake.
مقدمها الأيمن (muqaddamuha al-ayman): The front right side of the bier (when oriented towards the grave).
مؤخرها الأيمن (mu'akhkharuha al-ayman): The back right side of the bier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Suicide and Patricide: Funeral Rites in the Hanafi School
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on washing and performing the funeral prayer for a suicide and for one who kills a parent, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the suicide.

Core Idea
A suicide is washed and prayed over; a parent-killer is not—but the suicide ruling is disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from Nur al-Idah addresses two distinct cases: a person who commits suicide and a person who kills his father or mother. While both are washed, the funeral prayer is treated differently. The suicide presents an internal Hanafi disagreement, while the parent-killer is clearly excluded from the prayer. Understanding these rulings requires carefully distinguishing the definitions and the evidence cited.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases and their base rulings
The matn states: the suicide is washed and prayed upon; the intentional parent-killer is not prayed upon. Note that washing is affirmed for both unless otherwise specified.
The text does not explicitly state that the parent-killer is washed, but the default rule for all Muslims is that they are washed.
2
Define 'suicide' (qātil nafsihī) and 'parent-killer' (qātil aḥad abawayhi)
The commentator explains that suicide means intentional self-killing not due to severe pain. Accidental or coerced self-killing is excluded. Parent-killer means unjust killing of a parent; killing a Harbi or rebel parent incurs no sin.
This definition narrows the application of the rulings.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the funeral prayer for the suicide
Abu Yusuf holds that no prayer is performed, likening the suicide to a rebel (baghi) because of the element of oppression. The two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the prayer is performed because the suicide's blood is wasted (hader), so it is like a natural death.
The dispute is based on different analogies (qiyas) and a hadith.
4
Analyze the dalil cited for Abu Yusuf's position
The text quotes a hadith from Sahih Muslim: the Prophet did not pray over a man who killed himself with a dagger. This supports Abu Yusuf's view. The two companions rely on the principle that a Muslim's blood is inviolable but wasted, so the prayer is still due.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut), so it does not override the general evidence for prayer over a Muslim.
5
Understand the exception for the parent-killer
Even if the parent-killer repents, the funeral prayer is not performed because of the gravity of the crime. However, if the parent was a Harbi or a rebel, the killer is not sinful and normal rites apply.
This ruling is not subject to the same ikhtilaf as the suicide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقاتل نفسه يغسل ويصلى عليه لا على قاتل أحد أبويه عمدا. وقاتل نفسه: أراد به قاتل نفسه عمداً لا لشدة وجع، فخرج بمفهومه الخطأ فإنه يغسل ويصلى عليه. [مراقي الفلاح: ٦٠٢] ويصلى عليه أي من قتل نفسه عمداً اختلف فيه المشايخ قيل: يصلى عليه، وقيل: لا، ومنهم من حكى فيه خلافاً بين أبي يوسف وصاحبيه، فعنده: لا يصلى عليه وعندهما يصلى عليه، لأبي يوسف: إنه ظالم بالقتل، فيلحق بالباغي ولهما أن دمه هدر، فصار كما لو مات حتف أنفه، وفي صحيح مسلم ما يؤيد قول أبي يوسف: عن جابر بن سمرة قال: أتي النبي برجل قتل نفسه بمشاقص، فلم يصل عليه. [فتح القدير: ١٠٩/٢] قاتل أحد أبويه أراد به من قتل أباه أو أمه ظلماً؛ لأن من قتل أباه الحربي أو أمه الحربية أو أباه الباغي، فليس عليه شيء من الإثم
The one who kills himself is washed and prayed upon, but not the one who intentionally kills one of his parents. And [the commentator says] 'Qatilu nafsihi': He intended by it one who kills himself intentionally, not due to severe pain. So by its implication, the mistaken one is excluded, for he is washed and prayed upon. [Maraqi al-Falah: 602] And 'yusalla alayh' i.e., the one who kills himself intentionally – the scholars differed about it. Some said: he is prayed upon, and some said: no. And among them is one who narrated a difference between Abu Yusuf and his two companions. According to him (Abu Yusuf): he is not prayed upon, and according to them (the two) he is prayed upon. For Abu Yusuf: he is an oppressor by killing, so he is joined to the rebel. For the two: his blood is wasted, so it is as if he died naturally. And in Sahih Muslim is that which supports Abu Yusuf's saying: from Jabir ibn Samurah who said: The Prophet was brought a man who killed himself with a dagger, and he did not pray over him. [Fath al-Qadir: 2/109] 'Qatilu ahad abawayhi': He intended by it one who kills his father or mother unjustly; because one who kills his father who is a Harbi (enemy combatant) or his mother who is a Harbiyya, or his father who is a rebel (baghi), he is not sinful at all.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that a suicide is washed and prayed over, but one who kills his parent intentionally does not receive the funeral prayer. The commentator clarifies that 'suicide' refers to intentional self-killing not due to unbearable pain. For such a person, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf holds that the funeral prayer is not performed, likening the suicide to a rebel (baghi) whose prayer is not performed. The two companions (the Sahibayn – Abu Hanifa and Muhammad according to some) hold that the prayer is performed because the blood is wasted, so it is like a natural death. A hadith in Sahih Muslim supports Abu Yusuf: the Prophet did not pray over a suicide. For the parent-killer, the ruling is that he is not prayed over if the killing is intentional and unjust. However, if the parent is a Harbi (non-Muslim at war) or a rebel, there is no sin. The passage emphasizes the distinction between just and unjust killing of parents.

Key Terms
قاتل نفسه (qātilu nafsihi): A person who intentionally kills himself, not due to severe pain or coercion.
قاتل أحد أبويه (qātilu aḥadi abawayhi): A person who kills either of his parents unjustly.
باغي (bāghī): A rebel who fights against a just Muslim ruler.
حربي (ḥarbī): A non-Muslim who is in a state of war with Muslims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is washed but the prayer is not performed.
B) The prayer is performed unless the father was a rebel.
C) The prayer is not performed if the killing was intentional and unjust.
D) The prayer is always performed regardless of circumstances.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a suicide is washed and generally prayed upon (with a minority opposing), but an intentional parent-killer is not prayed upon unless the killing was justified.

Method of Burial: Lahd and Shaq
Ruling on burial method and actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the method of burial (lahd vs shaq) and explain the conditions for each.

Core Idea
Lahd is the sunnah method; shaq is only for soft ground.

When burying a deceased Muslim, the method of placing the body in the grave carries specific rulings in the Hanafi school. This passage from Nur al-Idah explains the preferred method (lahd) and the exception (shaq for soft ground), as well as the direction to face, the supplication, and the action of untying knots. Understanding these details ensures the burial is performed in accordance with the sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the preferred method and exception
State that lahd is the primary sunnah method for burial. Recognize that shaq is only permitted when the ground is soft (رخوة) and a lahd cannot be made.
This is a key practical distinction in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Note the actions during placement
List the recommended actions: enter the deceased from the qibla direction, turn the body onto its right side facing qibla, untie the knots of the shroud, and recite the designated supplication.
These actions are sunnah, not wajib, except facing qibla which has a difference of opinion.
3
Understand the ikhtilaf on facing qibla
Some Hanafi scholars hold that facing the qibla is wajib; others say it is sunnah. The preferred view (الأول أولى) is that it is sunnah.
The practical consequence: if omitted, it is not sinful according to the mufta bihi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويلحد ولا يشق إلا في أرض رخوة، ويدخل الميت من جهة القبلة، ويقول واضعه: [نديا في قبره] "بسم الله وعلى ملة رسول الله"، ويوجه إلى القبلة على جنبه الأيمن، وتحل العقد. ويلحد يقال: "لحد القبر" أي جعل فيه لحداً، وألحد "الميت وضعه في اللحد بفتح اللام كفلس، وبضمها كقفل، وجمع الأول لحود، والثاني الحاد، وهو حفيرة تجعل في جانب القبلة من القبر يوضع فيها الميت، وينصب عليها اللبن. ولا يشق إلخ: أي لا يشق بحفيرة في وسط القبر يوضع فيها الميت بعد حافتاه باللبن أو غيره، ثم يوضع الميت بينهما، ويسقف عليه باللبن أو الخشب، ولا يمس السقف الميت. من جهة القبلة: فتوضع الجنازة على القبر من جهة القبلة، ويحمله الآخذ مستقبلا حال الأخذ، ويضعه في اللحد لشرف القبلة. بسم الله إلخ: قال شمس الأئمة السرخسي: باسم الله وضعناك، وعلى ملة رسول الله سلمناك. ويوجه إلى القبلة وجوبا أو استينافا على اختلاف القولين. وتحل العقد ويقول الحال: لا تحرمنا أجره، ولا تفتنا بعده.
He should make a lahd (side niche) and not a shaq (central trench) except in soft ground. He should enter the deceased from the direction of the qibla. The one placing him should say: 'In the name of Allah and upon the religion of the Messenger of Allah'. He should be turned to face the qibla on his right side, and the knots (of the shroud) should be untied. It is said: 'lahd of the grave' means making a niche in it, and 'alhad' the deceased means placing him in the lahd...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage states the method of burial according to the Hanafi school. The preferred method is lahd (a niche dug in the side of the grave facing the qibla). Shaq (a trench in the middle) is only permitted when the ground is too soft for a lahd. The deceased is brought in from the qibla direction and placed in the lahd while saying a supplication. The body is positioned on its right side facing the qibla. The knots of the shroud are untied. There is a difference of opinion on whether facing the qibla is wajib or sunnah; the preferred view is sunnah. The person placing the body also says a prayer for reward and guidance.

Key Terms
لحد (lahd): A niche dug in the side of the grave facing the qibla, in which the deceased is placed.
شق (shaqq): A trench dug in the middle of the grave with walls on both sides, and the deceased is placed in between with a roof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Lahd (niche in the side of the qibla)
C) Placing the body directly on the ground
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prescribes lahd as the sunnah burial method, with shaq only allowed when the ground is soft.

Covering the Grave and Sprinkling Soil
Rulings on covering materials and sprinkling soil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on preferred and disliked materials for covering a grave, identify when tasjiyah is required, and describe the method of sprinkling soil.

Core Idea
Unbaked bricks and reeds are preferred; baked bricks and wood are disliked; only a woman's grave is covered with cloth before bricks.

This lesson presents three specific rulings related to the final stage of burial in the Hanafi school. First, the material used to cover the grave after the body is placed: unbaked clay bricks (labin) and reeds are preferred, while baked bricks and wood are discouraged (makruh tanzih). Second, for a female deceased, a cloth is spread over the grave (tasjiyah) to preserve modesty until the bricks are laid; this is not done for a male. Third, soil is sprinkled over the grave using hands, shovels, or any means possible. Understanding these details ensures that the burial is performed in accordance with the sunnah and Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its preferred/disliked materials
The primary act is covering the grave after placing the body. The text mentions that unbaked clay bricks (labin) and reeds (qasab) are arranged on it, while baked bricks (ajur) and wood (khashab) are disliked. Determine the hukm: the preferred materials are sunnah or mubah, while the disliked ones are makruh tanzih.
The ruling is a clear distinction between what is recommended and what is discouraged, not haram.
2
Understand the gender-specific ruling of tasjiyah
Tasjiyah refers to covering the grave with a cloth. It is performed only for a woman’s grave, until the bricks are laid over the lahd. For a man’s grave, tasjiyah is not done. This reflects the Hanafi concern for a woman’s modesty even after burial.
Al-Muhit notes that tasjiyah is unnecessary once the body is placed in the lahd.
3
Know the method of sprinkling soil
After covering the grave, soil is sprinkled (ihalat al-turab) using hands, shovels, or any available tool. The text emphasizes that sprinkling is done with the hands and other implements, indicating that any method is acceptable.
The key is that soil is poured, not just placed, onto the grave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسوى اللبن عليه والقصب وكره الآجر والخشب، وأن يسجى قبرها لا قبره، ويهال التراب. ويسوى اللبن بفتح اللام فيه وفي مفرده، وبكسر الباء فيهما، ومن العرب من يكسر اللام فيهما مع سكون الباء وهو كما في "الصحاح". ما يعمل من الطين مربعا ويبنى به. يسجى سجي الميت تسجية: مد عليه ثوبا وغطا به، ويسجى قبرها إلى أن يسوّى عليها اللحد، وفي "المحيط" : إذا وضعت في اللحد استغنى عن التسجية. ويهال يقال: هال عليه التراب يهيله صبّه. وفي حاشية الطحطاوي ويهال التراب في القبر بالأيدى وبالمساحي وبكل ما أمكن.
The bricks (made of unbaked clay) and reeds are arranged on it, while baked bricks and wood are disliked. And that the grave of a woman is covered (with a cloth) but not the grave of a man. And soil is sprinkled. The bricks are arranged... 'سجي' means to cover the deceased with a garment... Sprinkling soil: it is said 'haal alayhi al-turab' meaning to pour it. And in Hashiyat al-Tahtawi, soil is sprinkled in the grave with hands, shovels, and anything possible.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This ruling covers the material used to cover the grave after the body is placed. The preferred material is unbaked clay bricks (labin) and reeds. Baked bricks (ajur) and wood are disliked. Additionally, if the deceased is a woman, her grave should be covered with a cloth (tasjiyah) until the bricks are laid over her, but this is not done for a man. After covering, soil is sprinkled over the grave using hands or shovels. The tasjiyah is only needed until the body is placed in the lahd, as per al-Muhit.

Key Terms
لَبِن (labin): Unbaked clay bricks used for covering the grave.
تسجية (tasjiyah): Covering the grave of a woman with a cloth until the bricks are placed.
إهالة التراب (ihalat al-turab): Sprinkling soil over the grave after covering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Unbaked bricks and reeds are the preferred covering materials; baked bricks and wood are disliked; tasjiyah with a cloth is for women only; soil is sprinkled by hand.

The Shape of the Grave: Tasnim, Tarbi', and Building Prohibitions
Rulings on the shape and construction of graves

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on raising the grave and distinguish between the sunnah and wajib positions, and explain when building on a grave is haram versus makruh.

Core Idea
The grave should be raised hump-shaped; building for adornment is haram, for preservation is makruh.

When burying the deceased, the shape of the grave and any construction on it carries specific rulings. This passage from Nur al-Idah outlines the recommended form (tasnim) and the prohibition of building, but with nuances between adornment and preservation. We will also examine an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether raising the grave is obligatory or merely recommended.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: the shape of the grave (tasnim vs tarbi') and the act of building on graves. Identify the hukm level for each.
Tasnim is recommended; tarbi' is disliked; building for adornment is haram; for preservation is makruh tahrimi.
2
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on tasnim
Some Hanafi scholars consider tasnim wajib, others sunnah. The text prefers the sunnah view. Identify the reasoning behind each position.
The text says 'الأول أولى' indicating the sunnah is preferable.
3
Analyze the prohibition of building
Distinguish between building for adornment (haram) and for preservation (makruh tahrimi). Note the exception that before burial the site is not a grave, so building is not disliked.
The hadith from al-Khattabi prohibits plastering and domes, but later Hanafi texts allow plastering (tatyin) for preservation.
4
Apply the dalil
The dalil is a hadith reported by al-Khattabi: 'نهي عن تقصيص القبور وتكليلها'. Explain how this supports the prohibition of adornment but not necessarily preservation.
The prohibition is general, but the Hanafi school interprets it as applying to adornment, not essential maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسنم القبر ولا يربع ويحرم البناء عليه للزينة ويكره للإحكام بعد الدفن. ويسنم اختلفوا فيه فقيل بأولوية التسنيم وقيل: بوجوبها، والأول أولى، وهو أن يرفع القبر غير مسطح، ويجعله مرتفعا عن الأرض بقدر شبر وأكثر بقليل ولا بأس برش الماء حفظا له، ويكره أن يزيد على التراب الذي خرج منه، وعن محمد له: لا بأس بها. للإحكام إلخ ظاهر إطلاقه الكراهة أنها تحريمية، قال في "غريب الخطابي": نهي عن تقصيص القبور وتكليلها، و التقصيص التحصيص، والتكليل: بناء الكلل، وهي القباب والصوامع التي تبنى على القبر. بعد الدفن وأما قبل الدفن فليس بقبر، فلا يكره الدفن في مكان بني فيه وفي "النوازل": لا بأس بتطيينه، وفي "الغياثية": وعليه الفتوى.
The grave should be raised (musannam) and not made flat (murabba'). Building on it for adornment is haram, and for preservation (of the grave) is makruh after burial. Regarding raising: there is a difference, some say it is recommended, some say obligatory, and the first is preferable. It is to raise the grave not flat, making it elevated about a handspan or a little more. There is no harm in sprinkling water for preservation. It is disliked to add more soil than what came out of it. From Muhammad: there is no harm in it. For 'for preservation', the apparent general wording indicates it is makruh tahrimi. Al-Khattabi said: prohibition of plastering graves and putting domes on them... After burial, but before burial it is not a grave, so it is not disliked to bury in a place that was built. In al-Nawazil: there is no harm in plastering it, and in al-Ghiyathiyya: upon it is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage discusses the shape of the grave. The grave should be raised like a camel's hump (musannam), not flat like a square (murabba'). There is a difference of opinion on whether raising is obligatory or just recommended; the preferred view is that it is recommended. Raising is done by making it about a handspan high. Adding excessive soil is disliked. Building structures on graves for adornment is haram, but building for preservation (e.g., to protect the grave) is makruh tahrimi. Plastering the grave is permissible according to some. Before burial, the site is not considered a grave, so building there is not disliked.

Key Terms
تسنيم (tasnim): Raising the grave in a humped shape, about a handspan high.
تربيع (tarbi'): Making the grave flat and square, which is disliked.
تقصيص (taqsis): Plastering or whitewashing the grave.
تكليل (taklil): Building domes or structures (kalal) over graves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on raising the grave (tasnim) according to the preferred view in the Hanafi school?
A) Wajib
B) Sunnah muakkadah
C) Haram
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
The grave should be raised hump-shaped (tasnim) as a sunnah; building for adornment is haram, for preservation is makruh tahrimi.

Writing on Graves: Between Prohibition and Necessity
Ruling on writing on graves with need-based exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on writing on graves and identify when it is permissible due to need.

Core Idea
Writing on graves is permissible only to preserve the grave's trace; otherwise it is disliked.

This passage from Nur al-Idah discusses writing on graves. The general prohibition from a hadith is qualified by the necessity to identify the grave and prevent desecration. The Hanafi school permits writing when needed, but otherwise considers it disliked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general evidence
The text cites a hadith prohibiting writing on graves. Recognize that this is the default ruling.
Hadith from al-Bahr
2
Note the exception
Al-Muhit provides an exception: if there is need to preserve the trace and prevent desecration, writing is permitted.
Need is key
3
State the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that writing is permissible when needed, otherwise not.
From later fatwa works

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا بأس بالكتابة عليه لئلا يذهب الأثر ولا يمتهن. ولا بأس إلخ: قال في "البحر": الحديث المتقدم يمنع الكتابة، فليكن هو المعول عليه، لكن فصل في "المحيط": فقال: إن احتيج إلى الكتابة حتى لا يذهب الأثر ولا يمتهن به جازت، فأما الكتابة من غير عذر فلا. بالكتابة عليه: وهل قراءة القرآن عند القبور مكروهة، تكلموا فيه: قال أبو حنيفة الله يكره، وقال محمد: لا يكره، ومشايخنا أخذوا بقول محمد. لله رجل مات فأجلس وارثه رجلا يقرأ القرآن على قبره تكلموا فيه: منهم من كره ذلك، والمختار أنه ليس بمكروه، ويكون المأخوذ في هذا الباب قول محمد رحه، ولهذا حكي عن الشيخ أبي بكر العياض أنه أوصى عند موته بذلك، ولو كان مكروهاً لما أوصى به، هذا ما في "الشبلي" نقلا عن عرفت أن هذا الاختلاف في مجرد القراءة، فقال الإمام هو مكروه، وأما ما شاع في بلادنا الهندية لقراءة القرآن مع محدثات أخر فمكروه قطعا خلافا لمن جعل البدعات رزقه.
There is no harm in writing on it so that the trace is not lost and it is not desecrated. 'There is no harm etc.' says in al-Bahr: the previous hadith prohibits writing, so it should be relied upon, but al-Muhit makes a distinction: if there is need for writing so that the trace is not lost and it is not desecrated, it is permitted; otherwise, no. Regarding writing on it: and is recitation of Quran at graves disliked? They spoke about it: Abu Hanifa said it is disliked, and Muhammad said it is not disliked. Our scholars adopted Muhammad's view. A man died and his heir hired a man to recite Quran at his grave; they spoke about it, some disliked it, but the chosen view is that it is not disliked, and the adopted view in this matter is Muhammad's statement. Hence Sh. Abu Bakr al-Ayyad recommended it at his death. This is in al-Shabli. Know that this difference is about mere recitation; Imam Abu Hanifa said it is disliked. As for what became common in India of reciting Quran with other innovations, it is definitely disliked, contrary to those who make bid'at their livelihood.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: writing on graves and reciting Quran at graves. On writing, the general prohibition is from a hadith, but al-Muhit permits it if needed to preserve the grave's location and prevent desecration. The default is dislike unless there is necessity. On Quran recitation, Abu Hanifa held it as makruh, while Muhammad considered it not makruh. The later scholars (Mashayikh) adopted Muhammad's view, so it is not disliked. The chosen view is that mere recitation is not makruh. However, in later times, especially in India, recitation accompanied by innovations (bid'at) is definitely makruh.

Key Terms
كتابة على القبر (kitabah 'ala al-qabr): Writing on the grave, such as the deceased's name or Quranic verses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is writing on a grave permissible?
A) Only when writing Quranic verses
B) When there is a need to preserve the grave's trace
C) Always permissible
D) Never permissible

Key Takeaway
Writing on graves is permissible only when necessary to preserve the grave's location.

Reciting Quran at Graves: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on Quran recitation at graves with resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on reciting Quran at graves and state the preferred ruling.

Core Idea
The preferred Hanafi view is that reciting Quran at graves is not disliked, though Abu Hanifa held it as makruh.

This passage addresses the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani on reciting Quran at graves. Later scholars adopted Muhammad's view, making it the mufta bihi. The text also warns against mixing recitation with innovations.

1
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa declared it makruh; Muhammad said it is not makruh. Recognize the difference.
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
2

Tarjih authority: mashayikh
3

Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Ayyad willed recitation at his grave, indicating it is not disliked.
Practice of a prominent scholar
